       (i.e., in one of the equinoctial points on the
       <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">
        ecliptic
       </a>
       ), but the shadow falls above or below the opposite side of the ring when the Sun is south or north of the equator. Ptolemy quotes (in
       <i>
        Almagest
       </i>
       III.1 (H195)) a description by Hipparchus of an equatorial ring in Alexandria; a little further he describes two such instruments present in Alexandria in his own time.
      </p>
      <p>
       Hipparchus applied his knowledge of spherical angles to the problem of denoting locations on the Earth's surface. Before him a grid system had been used by
       <a href="/wiki/Dicaearchus" title="Dicaearchus">
        Dicaearchus
       </a>
       of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Messina,_Italy" title="Messina, Italy">
        Messana
       </a>
       , but Hipparchus was the first to apply mathematical rigor to the determination of the
       <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">
        latitude
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">
        longitude
       </a>
       of places on the Earth. Hipparchus wrote a critique in three books on the work of the geographer
       <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">
        Eratosthenes
       </a>
       of Cyrene (3rd century
       <span class="smallcaps">
        <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
         BC
        </span>
       </span>
       ), called
       <i>
        Pròs tèn 'Eratosthénous geografían
       </i>
       ("Against the Geography of Eratosthenes"). It is known to us from
       <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">
        Strabo
       </a>
       of Amaseia, who in his turn criticised Hipparchus in his own
       <i>
        Geografia
       </i>
       . Hipparchus apparently made many detailed corrections to the locations and distances mentioned by Eratosthenes. It seems he did not introduce many improvements in methods, but he did propose a means to determine the
       <a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">
        geographical longitudes
       </a>
       of different
       <a href="/wiki/City" title="City">
        cities
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">
        lunar eclipses
       </a>
       (Strabo
       <i>
        Geografia
       </i>
       1 January 2012). A lunar eclipse is visible simultaneously on half of the Earth, and the difference in longitude between places can be computed from the difference in local time when the eclipse is observed. His approach would give accurate results if it were correctly carried out but the limitations of timekeeping accuracy in his era made this method impractical.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Star_catalog">
        Star catalog
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hipparchus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Star catalog">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:239px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hipparchus_by_Raphael.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="180" data-file-width="237" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7e/Hipparchus_by_Raphael.jpg" width="237"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hipparchus_by_Raphael.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Hipparchus holding his celestial globe, in
         <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">
          Raphael
         </a>
         's
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/The_School_of_Athens" title="The School of Athens">
           The School of Athens
          </a>
         </i>
         (c. 1510)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Late in his career (possibly about 135
       <span class="smallcaps">
        <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
         BC
        </span>
       </span>
       ) Hipparchus compiled his star catalog, the original of which does not survive. He also constructed a celestial globe depicting the constellations, based on his observations. His interest in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fixed_star" title="Fixed star">
        fixed stars
       </a>
       may have been inspired by the observation of a
       <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">
        supernova
       </a>
       (according to Pliny), or by his discovery of precession, according to Ptolemy, who says that Hipparchus could not reconcile his data with earlier observations made by
       <a href="/wiki/Timocharis" title="Timocharis">
        Timocharis
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristillus" title="Aristillus">
        Aristillus
       </a>
       . For more information see
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discovery_of_precession" title="Discovery of precession">
        Discovery of precession
       </a>
       . In
       <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">
        Raphael
       </a>
       's painting
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_School_of_Athens" title="The School of Athens">
         The School of Athens
        </a>
       </i>
       , Hipparchus is depicted holding his celestial globe, as the representative figure for astronomy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Previously,
       <a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cnidus" title="Eudoxus of Cnidus">
        Eudoxus of Cnidus
       </a>
       in the 4th century
       <span class="smallcaps">
        <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
         BC
        </span>
       </span>
       had described the stars and constellations in two books called
       <i>
        Phaenomena
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Entropon
       </i>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Aratus" title="Aratus">
        Aratus
       </a>
       wrote a poem called
       <i>
        Phaenomena
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Arateia
       </i>
       based on Eudoxus's work. Hipparchus wrote a commentary on the
       <i>
        Arateia
       </i>
       – his only preserved work – which contains many stellar positions and times for rising, culmination, and setting of the constellations, and these are likely to have been based on his own measurements.
      </p>
      <p>
       Hipparchus made his measurements with an
       <a href="/wiki/Armillary_sphere" title="Armillary sphere">
        armillary sphere
       </a>
       , and obtained the positions of at least 850 stars. It is disputed which coordinate system(s) he used. Ptolemy's catalog in the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">
         Almagest
        </a>
       </i>
       , which is derived from Hipparchus's catalog, is given in
       <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic_coordinate_system" title="Ecliptic coordinate system">
        ecliptic coordinates
       </a>
       . However Delambre in his
       <i>
        Histoire de l'Astronomie Ancienne
       </i>
       (1817) concluded that Hipparchus knew and used the
       <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_coordinate_system" title="Equatorial coordinate system">
        equatorial coordinate system
       </a>
       , a conclusion challenged by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otto_Neugebauer" title="Otto Neugebauer">
        Otto Neugebauer
       </a>
       in his
       <i>
        A History of Ancient Mathematical Astronomy
       </i>
       (1975). Hipparchus seems to have used a mix of
       <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic_coordinate_system" title="Ecliptic coordinate system">
        ecliptic coordinates
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_coordinate_system" title="Equatorial coordinate system">
        equatorial coordinates
       </a>
       : in his commentary on Eudoxos he provides stars' polar distance (equivalent to the
       <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">
        declination
       </a>
       in the equatorial system), right ascension (equatorial), longitude (ecliptical), polar longitude (hybrid), but not celestial latitude.
      </p>
      <p>
       As with most of his work, Hipparchus's star catalog was adopted and perhaps expanded by Ptolemy. Delambre, in 1817, cast doubt on Ptolemy's work. It was disputed whether the star catalog in the
       <i>
        Almagest
       </i>
       is due to Hipparchus, but 1976–2002 statistical and spatial analyses (by
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Russell_Newton" title="Robert Russell Newton">
        R. R. Newton
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Rawlins" title="Dennis Rawlins">
        Dennis Rawlins
       </a>
       , Gerd Grasshoff,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Keith Pickering
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and Dennis Duke
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) have shown conclusively that the
       <i>
        Almagest
       </i>
       star catalog is almost entirely Hipparchan. Ptolemy has even (since Brahe, 1598) been accused by astronomers of fraud for stating (
       <i>
        Syntaxis
       </i>
       , book 7, chapter 4) that he observed all 1025 stars: for almost every star he used Hipparchus's data and precessed it to his own epoch ​
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        2
        <span class="visualhide">
        </span>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         3
        </sub>
       </span>
       centuries later by adding 2°40' to the longitude, using an erroneously small precession constant of 1° per century.
      </p>
      <p>
       In any case the work started by Hipparchus has had a lasting heritage, and was much later updated by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%27Abd_Al-Rahman_Al_Sufi" title="'Abd Al-Rahman Al Sufi">
        Al Sufi
       </a>
       (964) and Copernicus (1543).
       <a href="/wiki/Ulugh_Beg" title="Ulugh Beg">
        Ulugh Beg
       </a>
       reobserved all the Hipparchus stars he could see from Samarkand in 1437 to about the same accuracy as Hipparchus's. The catalog was superseded only in the late 16th century by Brahe and Wilhelm IV of Kassel via superior ruled instruments and spherical trigonometry, which improved accuracy by an order of magnitude even before the invention of the telescope. Hipparchus is considered the greatest observational astronomer from classical antiquity until Brahe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Stellar_magnitude">
        Stellar magnitude
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hipparchus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Stellar magnitude">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Hipparchus ranked stars in three
       <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">
        magnitude
       </a>
       very general classes according to their brightness but he did not assign a numerical brightness value to any star.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The magnitude system ranging from 1 (brightest) to 6 (faintest) was established by Ptolemy.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       That system by Ptolemy is effectively still in use today, though extended and made more precise through the introduction of a logarithmic scale by
       <a href="/wiki/N._R._Pogson" title="N. R. Pogson">
        N. R. Pogson
       </a>
       in 1856.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Precession_of_the_equinoxes_.28146.E2.80.93127_BC.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Precession_of_the_equinoxes_(146–127_BC)">
        Precession of the equinoxes (146–127
        <span class="smallcaps">
         <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
          BC
         </span>
        </span>
        )
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hipparchus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Precession of the equinoxes (146–127 BC)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precession_(astronomy)" title="Precession (astronomy)">
        Precession (astronomy)
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Hipparchus is generally recognized as discoverer of the
       <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">
        precession
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">
        equinoxes
       </a>
       in 127
       <span class="smallcaps">
        <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
         BC
        </span>
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       His two books on precession,
       <i>
        On the Displacement of the Solsticial and Equinoctial Points
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        On the Length of the Year
       </i>
       , are both mentioned in the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">
         Almagest
        </a>
       </i>
       of Claudius
       <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
        Ptolemy
       </a>
       . According to Ptolemy, Hipparchus measured the longitude of
       <a href="/wiki/Spica" title="Spica">
        Spica
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Regulus" title="Regulus">
        Regulus
       </a>
       and other bright stars. Comparing his measurements with data from his predecessors,
       <a href="/wiki/Timocharis" title="Timocharis">
        Timocharis
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristillus" title="Aristillus">
        Aristillus
       </a>
       , he concluded that Spica had moved 2° relative to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autumnal_equinox" title="Autumnal equinox">
        autumnal equinox
       </a>
       . He also compared the lengths of the
       <a href="/wiki/Tropical_year" title="Tropical year">
        tropical year
       </a>
       (the time it takes the Sun to return to an equinox) and the
       <a href="/wiki/Sidereal_year" title="Sidereal year">
        sidereal year
       </a>
       (the time it takes the Sun to return to a fixed star), and found a slight discrepancy. Hipparchus concluded that the equinoxes were moving ("precessing") through the zodiac, and that the rate of precession was not less than 1° in a century.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
        Geography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hipparchus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Geography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Hipparchus's treatise "Against the Geography of
       <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">
        Eratosthenes
       </a>
       " in three books is not preserved.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most of our knowledge of it comes from
       <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">
        Strabo
       </a>
       , according to whom Hipparchus thoroughly and often unfairly criticized
       <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">
        Eratosthenes
       </a>
       mainly for internal contradictions and inaccuracy in determining positions of geographical localities. Hipparchus insists that a geographic map must be based only on astronomical measurements of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latitude_and_longitude" title="Latitude and longitude">
        latitudes and longitudes
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Triangulation" title="Triangulation">
        triangulation
       </a>
       for finding unknown distances. In geographic theory and methods Hipparchus introduced three main innovations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He was the first to use the
       <a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">
        grade grid
       </a>
       , to determine
       <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">
        geographic latitude
       </a>
       from star observations, and not only from the sun’s altitude, a method known long before him, and to suggest that
       <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">
        geographic longitude
       </a>
       could be determined by means of simultaneous observations of lunar eclipses in distant places. In the practical part of his work, the so-called "table of
       <a href="/wiki/Clime" title="Clime">
        climata
       </a>
       ", Hipparchus listed latitudes for several tens of localities. In particular, he improved
       <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">
        Eratosthenes
       </a>
       ' values for the latitudes of
       <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">
        Athens
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">
        Sicily
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comorin" title="Comorin">
        southern extremity of India
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In calculating latitudes of
       <a href="/wiki/Clime" title="Clime">
        climata
       </a>
       (latitudes correlated with the length of the longest solstitial day), Hipparchus used an unexpectedly accurate value for
       <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">
        the obliquity of the ecliptic
       </a>
       , 23°40′ (the actual value in the second half of the 2nd century
       <span class="smallcaps">
        <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">
         BC
        </span>
       </span>
       was approximately 23°43′), whereas all other ancient authors knew only a roughly rounded value 24°, and even
       <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
        Ptolemy
       </a>
       used a less accurate value, 23°51′.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Hipparchus opposed the view generally accepted in the
       <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">
        Hellenistic period
       </a>
       that the
       <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">
        Atlantic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">
        Indian Oceans
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">
        Caspian Sea
       </a>
       are parts of a single ocean. At the same time he extends the limits of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ecumene" title="Ecumene">
        oikoumene
       </a>
       , i.e. the inhabited part of the land, up to the
       <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">
        equator
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">
        Arctic Circle
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Hipparchus’ ideas found their reflection in the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographia_(Ptolemy)" title="Geographia (Ptolemy)">
         Geography
        </a>
       </i>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
        Ptolemy
       </a>
       . In essence, Ptolemy's work is an extended attempt to realize Hipparchus’ vision of what geography ought to be.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">
        Legacy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hipparchus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Legacy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The rather cumbersome formal name for the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">
        ESA
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Hipparcos" title="Hipparcos">
        Hipparcos Space Astrometry Mission
       </a>
       was High Precision Parallax Collecting Satellite; it was deliberately named in this way to give an acronym, HiPParCoS, that echoed and commemorated the name of Hipparchus. The lunar crater
       <a href="/wiki/Hipparchus_(lunar_crater)" title="Hipparchus (lunar crater)">
        Hipparchus
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroid
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/4000_Hipparchus" title="4000 Hipparchus">
        4000 Hipparchus
       </a>
       are more directly named after him.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Monument">
        Monument
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hipparchus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Monument">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The Astronomer's Monument at the
       <a href="/wiki/Griffith_Observatory" title="Griffith Observatory">
        Griffith Observatory
       </a>
       in Los Angeles, California, United States features a relief of Hipparchus as one of six of the greatest astronomers of all time and the only one from Antiquity.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Editions_and_translations">
        Editions and translations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

        </a>

         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>

        <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=1qNDAAAAcAAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;dq=berger+hugo+hipparchus&amp;source=bl&amp;ots=4yHbGolIRN&amp;sig=gOAsU8DfieuAUEJKiHO4bW198nA&amp;hl=ru&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=telmUKShJ66N4gSTo4GoAQ&amp;ved=0CDQQ6AEwAQ#v=onepage&amp;q=berger%20hugo%20hipparchus&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">

           <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Centaurus&amp;rft.atitle=On+the+Babylonian+origin+of+some+Hipparchian+parameters&amp;rft.volume=4&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.pages=122-125&amp;rft.date=1955&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1111%2Fj.1600-0498.1955.tb00619.x&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1955Cent....4..122A&amp;rft.aulast=Aaboe&amp;rft.aufirst=Asger&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHipparchus">

           . On p. 124, Aaboe identifies the Hipparchian equation 5458 syn. mo. = 5923 drac. mo. with the equation of 1,30,58 syn. mo. = 1,38,43 drac. mo. (written in

           , London 1955.

             ^

          <span class="reference-text">
           <cite class="citation" id="CITEREFLeverington2003">
            Leverington, David (2003),
            <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=6Hpi202ybn8C&amp;pg=PA30" rel="nofollow">
             <i>
              Babylon to Voyager and Beyond: A History of Planetary Astronomy
             </i>
            </a>
            , Cambridge University Press, p. 30,
            <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
             ISBN
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780521808408" title="Special:BookSources/9780521808408">
             9780521808408
            </a>
           </cite>
           <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Babylon+to+Voyager+and+Beyond%3A+A+History+of+Planetary+Astronomy&amp;rft.pages=30&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=9780521808408&amp;rft.aulast=Leverington&amp;rft.aufirst=David&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D6Hpi202ybn8C%26pg%3DPA30&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHipparchus">
            <span style="display:none;">
            </span>
           </span>

          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-33">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-33">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           <a class="external text" href="http://www.dioi.org/vols/w11.pdf" rel="nofollow">
            <i>
             DIO
            </i>
           </a>
           , volume 1, number 1, pages 49–66; A.Jones, 2001; Thurston,
           <i>
            op. cit.
           </i>
           , page 62
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-34">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-34">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Thurston,
           <i>
            op. cit.
           </i>
           , page 67, note 16. R. Newton proposed that Hipparchus made an error of a degree in one of the trios' eclipses. D.Rawlins's theory (Thurston
           <i>
            op. cit.
           </i>
           ) that Hipparchus analysed the two trios in pairs not threesomes provides a possible explanation for the one degree slip. It was a
           <a class="external text" href="http://www.dioi.org/cot.htm#qtpt" rel="nofollow">
            fudge
           </a>
           necessitated by inadequacies of analysing by pairs instead of using the better method Ptolemy applies at
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">
             Almagest
            </a>
           </i>
           Book 4 Parts 6 and 11.
          </span>
